________________
Chapter 1 : Plotting for exploratory data analysis (EDA)
________________




Basic Terminology and Iris Flower dataset:


         * EDA: Simple analysis to understand data, tools include statistics, linear algebra and
         * plotting tools
         * Features: Sepal Length, Sepal Width, Petal length, Petal Width.
         * Columns: Feature/Variable/Input-variable/independent-variable
         * Rows: Data points or vector: n-dimensional numerical variable
         * Species: Label/dependent-variable/Output-variable/Class-label/Response label
         * Steps to approach EDA baby steps


  



         * Vector: 2-D, 3-D, 4-D,.... n-D
         * What is a 1-D vector: Scalar.  
         * Toy Dataset: Iris Dataset
            * A simple dataset to learn the basics.
            * 3 flowers of Iris species.
            * 1936 by Ronald Fisher.
            * Objective: Classify a new flower as belonging to one of the 3 classes given the 4 features.
            * Importance of domain knowledge : for analysing data you have to think like a person of that domain knowledge.


Q) Why use petal and sepal dimensions as features?Why do we not use 'colour' as a feature?
⇒ because a botanist knows that for identifying the species the sepal and petal length and width are important so you have to know or think or consult a domain knowledge person.


import pandas as pd
import seaborn as sns
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import numpy as np
​


#download iris.csv
#Load Iris.csv into a pandas dataFrame.
iris = pd.read_csv("iris.csv")
​
# (Q) how many data-points and features?
print (iris.shape)


#(Q) What are the column names in our dataset?
print (iris.columns)
Index(['sepal_length', 'sepal_width', 'petal_length', 'petal_width',
       'species'],
      dtype='object')


#(Q) How many data points for each class are present?
#(or) How many flowers for each species are present?
iris["species"].value_counts()


# balanced-dataset vs imbalanced datasets
#Iris is a balanced dataset as the number of data points for every class is 50 and for an imbalance data set the difference between two classes is a big small gap may be called as balanced.
virginica     50
versicolor    50
setosa        50
Name: species, dtype: int64


2-D Scatter Plot


#2-D scatter plot:
#ALWAYS understand the axis: labels and scale.
​
iris.plot(kind='scatter', x='sepal_length', y='sepal_width') 
plt.show()  
​
#cannot make much sense out of it.
#What if we color the points by their class-label/flower-type.
# 2-D Scatter plot with color-coding for each flower type/class.
# Here 'sns' corresponds to seaborn.
sns.set_style("whitegrid");
sns.FacetGrid(iris, hue="species", height=4) \
  .map(plt.scatter, "sepal_length", "sepal_width") \
  .add_legend();  
plt.show();
​
# Notice that the blue points can be easily separated called linear separable
# from red and green by drawing a line.
# But red and green data points cannot be easily separated.
# Can we draw multiple 2-D scatter plots for each combination of features?
# How many combinations exist? 4C2 = 6.

 4C2 = 4!/ [2! (4 – 2)!]
= 4!/ (2! 2!)= (4 × 3 × 2 × 1)/ (2 × 1 × 2 × 1)
=6


Observation(s):
            1. Using sepal_length and sepal_width features, we can distinguish Setosa flowers from others.
            2. Separating Versicolor from Virginica is much harder as they have considerable overlap.


Pair-plot  
# pairwise scatter plot: Pair-Plot
# Dis-advantages:
##Can be used when the number of features are high.
##Cannot visualise higher dimensional patterns in 3-D and 4-D.
#Only possible to view 2D patterns.
sns.set_style("whitegrid");
sns.pairplot(iris, hue="species", height=3);
plt.show()
# NOTE: the diagonal elements are PDFs for each feature. PDFs are explained below.


Observations
            1. petal_length and petal_width are the most useful features to identify various flower types.
            2. While Setosa can be easily identified (linearly separable), Virnica and Versicolor have some overlap (almost linearly separable).
            3. We can find "lines" and "if-else" conditions to build a simple model to classify the flower types.




Histogram, PDF, CDF


# What about a 1-D scatter plot using just one feature?
#1-D scatter plot of petal-length
import numpy as np
iris_setosa = iris.loc[iris["species"] == "setosa"];
iris_virginica = iris.loc[iris["species"] == "virginica"];
iris_versicolor = iris.loc[iris["species"] == "versicolor"];
#print(iris_setosa["petal_length"])
plt.plot(iris_setosa["petal_length"], np.zeros_like(iris_setosa['petal_length']), 'o')
plt.plot(iris_versicolor["petal_length"], np.zeros_like(iris_versicolor['petal_length']), 'o')
plt.plot(iris_virginica["petal_length"], np.zeros_like(iris_virginica['petal_length']), 'o')
​
plt.show()
  

#Disadvantages of 1-D scatter plot: Very hard 
to make sense as points
#are overlapping a lot.
#Are there better ways of visualising 1-D scatter plots?




sns.FacetGrid(iris, hue="species", height=5) \
  .map(sns.distplot, "petal_length") \
  .add_legend();
plt.show();
  

This is called histogram and also called density plot.
Where x axis is our variable and y axis
Is the count






sns.FacetGrid(iris, hue="species", size=5) \
  .map(sns.distplot, "petal_width") \
  .add_legend();

  .map(sns.distplot, "sepal_length") \
  .add_legend();
plt.show();
  







sns.FacetGrid(iris, hue="species", size=5) \
  .map(sns.distplot, "sepal_width") \
  .add_legend();
plt.show();
  









            * PDF: smoothed form of histogram (Kernel Density Estimation)
            * Univariate Analysis using PDF:analysing one variable from the given dataset from above four we can conclude that petal_length is the best feature for identifying flowers.
            * sns.FacetGrid(df, hue = 'species', height= 5, palette="Blues")\
    .map(sns.histplot ,'petal_length', kde = True)\
    .add_legend()
plt.show()
            *  Distplot is soon going to deprecated in future by seaborn.(so use histplot)




# Histograms and Probability Density Functions (PDF) using KDE
# How to compute PDFs using counts/frequencies of data points in each window.
# How window width affects the PDF plot.


# Interpreting a PDF:
## Why is it called a density plot?
## Why is it called a probability plot?
## for each value of petal_length, what does the value on the y-axis mean?
# Notice that we can write a simple if..else condition as if(petal_length) < 2.5 then flower type is setosa.
# Using just one feature, we can build a simple "model" using if..else... statements.
​
# Disadv of PDF: Can we say what percentage of versicolor points have a petal_length of less than 5?
​
# Do some of these plots look like a bell-curve you studied in under-grad?
# Gaussian/Normal distribution.
# What is "normal" about normal distribution?
# e.g: Heights of male students in a class.
# One of the most frequent distributions in nature.




# Need for Cumulative Distribution Function (CDF)
# We can visually see what percentage of versicolor flowers have a petal_length of less than 1.6?
# How to construct a CDF?
# How to read a CDF?​
#Plot CDF of petal_length
​
counts, bin_edges = np.histogram(iris_setosa['petal_length'], bins=10,
                                density = True)
pdf = counts/(sum(counts))
print(pdf);
print(bin_edges)
​
#compute CDF
#cumsum:: cumulative sum
cdf = np.cumsum(pdf)
plt.plot(bin_edges[1:],pdf)
plt.plot(bin_edges[1:], cdf)
plt.show();

Notes: 
            1. the area under the curve till that point of pdf is cdf.
            2. Differentiation of cdf is pdf.
            3. Integration of pdf is cdf.
            4. Cumulative sum is sum of all the values from start to that point.




# Plots of CDF of petal_length for various types of flowers.
# Misclassification error if you use petal_length only.
​
counts, bin_edges = np.histogram(iris_setosa['petal_length'], bins=10,
                                density = True)
pdf = counts/(sum(counts))
cdf = np.cumsum(pdf)
plt.plot(bin_edges[1:],pdf)
plt.plot(bin_edges[1:], cdf)

Mean, Variance and Std-dev


  

Mean measures the central tendency.


  

Fig1: variance        
​
  

Fig2: standard deviation


= variance
 = standard deviation
xi = the value of one observation
,𝜇 = the mean value of all observation
 = number of observation




            * The variance is a measure of variability. It is calculated by taking the average of squared deviations from the mean.
            * Variance tells you the degree of spread in your data set. The more spread the data, the larger the variance is in relation to the mean.
Variance vs standard deviation
            * The standard deviation is derived from variance and tells you, on average, how far each value lies from the mean. It’s the square root of variance.   
OR
               *  The width of the spread of the values on an axis.


               * Both measures reflect variability in a distribution, but their units differ:
               * Standard deviation is expressed in the same units as the original values (e.g., meters).
               * Variance is expressed in much larger units (e.g., meters squared)
               * Since the units of variance are much larger than those of a typical value of a data set, it’s harder to interpret the variance number intuitively. That’s why standard deviation is often preferred as a main measure of variability.
               * However, the variance is more informative about variability than the standard deviation, and it’s used in making statistical inferences.
               * Mean, variance and standard deviation are corrupted if an outlier exists.




print("Means:")
print(np.mean(iris_setosa["petal_length"]))
#Mean with an outlier(a wrong value which can drastically change the outcome) here 50 is outlier.
print(np.mean(np.append(iris_setosa["petal_length"],50)));


print(np.mean(iris_virginica["petal_length"]))
print(np.mean(iris_versicolor["petal_length"]));


print("\nStd-dev:");
print(np.std(iris_setosa["petal_length"]))
print(np.std(iris_virginica["petal_length"]))
print(np.std(iris_versicolor["petal_length"]))

Median: 
               * Median does not get corrupted due to the presence of outliers. better statistic for central
tendency.
               * Sort the list in an increasing order and pick the middle value if the number of list is even number then find the average of two middle values.
               * Median value is corrupt if you have an outlier more than 50%.(because then the outlier is the middle point or avg of middle points which then affect the median).




#Median
print("\nMedians:")
print(np.median(iris_setosa["petal_length"]))


#Median with an outlier
print(np.median(np.append(iris_setosa["petal_length"],50)));
print(np.median(iris_virginica["petal_length"]))
print(np.median(iris_versicolor["petal_length"]))

Percentiles, Quantiles
               * Basically the 95th percentile says that 95% of the time, the points are below this amount and 5% of the time, the points are above this amount.
               * A 50th percentile is the same as a "median." An average, or "mean," is similar but a weighted result.
               *  A 95th percentile is 4 days and 100 percentile is 5.5 days for delivery:  says that 95% of the time delivery reaches the customer within 4 days and 100 percent gets its delivery within 5.5 days.
               * 25th ,50th,75th,100th percentile are called quantiles.




print("\nQuantiles:")
print(np.percentile(iris_setosa["petal_length"],np.arange(0, 100, 25)))
print(np.percentile(iris_virginica["petal_length"],np.arange(0, 100, 25)))
print(np.percentile(iris_versicolor["petal_length"], np.arange(0, 100, 25)))

print(np.percentile(iris_versicolor["petal_length"], 90))

MAD(Median Absolute Deviation) :
               * The median absolute deviation of a dataset is the average distance between each data point and the median. It gives us an idea about the variability in a dataset.
               * How far away are my points from the central tendency. 
               * Unlike standard deviation which use mean and affected by outlier, the MAD has no problem with outliers.


  




​
from statsmodels import robust
print ("\nMedian Absolute Deviation")
print(robust.mad(iris_setosa["petal_length"]))
print(robust.mad(iris_virginica["petal_length"]))
print(robust.mad(iris_versicolor["petal_length"]))

Violin plots:
A violin plot is a method of plotting numeric data. It is similar to a box plot, with the
addition of a rotated kernel density plot on each side. Violin plots are similar to boxplots, except that they also show the probability density of the data at different values,
usually smoothed by a kernel density estimator.


# A violin plot combines the benefits 
of the previous two plots
#and simplifies them
​#Inside the violin plot there is a box plot with a white dot inside(50 percentile) and outside there is a pdf or bell curve.


  



# Denser regions of the data are fatter, 
and sparser ones thinner
#in a violin plot
​
sns.violinplot(x="species", y="petal_length", data=iris, size=8)
plt.show()
  

Summarising plots in english
               * Explain your findings/conclusions in plain english
               * Never forget your objective (the problem you are solving) .
               * Perform all of your EDA aligned with your objectives.
               * Univariate analysis:
               * When we analysis in only one variable.e.g: petal_length
               * Box-plot,violin plot, pdf, cdf , histogram etc.
               * Bivariate analysis:
               * When we analyze in two variable.e.g: petal_length, petal_width.
               * Pair plots, scatter plots etc.
               * multivariate analysis:
               * When we analyze more than 2 variables.
               * 3D plots.

Combining probability densities of two variables.dense regions are darker as if a hill is coming out.And this is called contour probability density plot.






How to approach EDA(Steps in Data Exploration and Preprocessing):


               1. Identification of variables and data types
               1. Numerical and Categorical
               2. the next step is to identify the Predictor(Inputs) and Target (output) variables.
               3. We have to convert the features in the following target values e.g we have to convert DOB from objects to DateTime type.
               2. Analyzing the basic metrics
               1. Pandas describe() is used to view some basic statistical details like count,percentiles, mean, std, and maximum value of a data frame or a series of numeric values. As it gives the count of each variable, we can identify the missing values using this method.
               3. Non-Graphical Univariate Analysis
               1. The value_counts() method in Pandas returns a series containing the counts of all the unique values in a column. The output will be in descending order so that the first element is the most frequently-occurring element.
               2. The nunique() function in Pandas returns a number with distinct observations in a column.
               3. Similarly, the unique() function of pandas returns the list of unique values in the dataset.
               4. When we import our dataset from a CSV file, many blank columns are imported as null values into the Data Frame, which can later create problems while operating that data frame. Pandas isnull() method is used to check and manage NULL values in a data frame.
               1. train.apply(lambda x: sum(x.isnull()),axis=0) // this tells that how many null value are there in each column.
               5. Pandas astype() method is used to change the data type of a column. And to_datetime() method is used to change, particularly to DateTime type. When the data frame is imported from a CSV file, the data type of the columns is set automatically, which many times is not what it actually should have.so we have to convert it to our desire datatype.e.g
               1. train['Date.of.Birth']= pd.to_datetime(train['Date.of.Birth'])
               2. train['ltv'] = train['ltv'].astype('int64')
               4. Graphical Univariate Analysis
               1.                5. Bivariate Analysis
               6. Variable transformations
               7. Missing value treatment
               8. Outlier treatment
               9. Correlation Analysis
               10. Dimensionality Reduction

Introduction


Why Learn it?
               * We will apply it to solve specific type of problems in ML; we will learn things in 2d and 3d and extend it to nd


Introduction to Vectors (2-D, 3-D, n-D), Row Vector and Column Vector Point:
               * Let us have a Coordinate system with x1 and x2 axes. Point can be denoted by n-dimensional vector:


Distance of a point from origin:
               * In 2D: sqrt(x12 + x22 )
               * In 3D: sqrt(x12 + x22 +x32 )
               * In nD: sqrt(x12 + x22 +x32 +....+xn2 )


Distance between two points:
               * In 2D: sqrt((x1-y1) 2 + (x2-y2)2 )
               * In 2D: sqrt((x1-y1)2 + (x2-y2)2 + (x3-y3)2 )
               * In 2D: sqrt((x1-y1)2 + (x2-y2)2 + ...+ (xn-yn)2 )
               * Row vector: A matrix with n columns and 1 row
               * Column Vector: A matrix with 1 column and n rows


Dot Product(scalar product) and Angle between 2 Vectors
               * a = [a1, a2, ......., an]
               * b = [b1, b2, ......, bn]
               * c = a + b = [a1 + b1, a2 + b2, ....., an + bn]
               * Dot product: a.b = a1b1 + a2b2 + ..... anbn = aT * b (T : transpose of a vector)
               * by default every vector is a column vector


Geometrically represents the angle between the two vectors:  
               * a.b = ||a|| ||b|| cos(θ) = a1b1 + a2b2 +.....
||a|| = sqrt ( a12 + a22 + .....)


               * If the dot product of two vectors is zero then the two vectors are orthogonal and the angle between two vectors is 900.














Projection and Unit Vector
               * Projection of a on b = d = a cos(θ) =   


  





                  * Unit Vector:  = 
                  * Unit vector  is in the same direction as .
                  * Projection is same as flashing torch light in a and the shadow of a on b is projection of a in b
                  *  |||| = 1






Equation of a line (2-D), Plane (3-D) and Hyperplane (n-D), Plane passing through origin, Normal to a Plane:


                     * If there is a vector example: X then we treat it as a column vector if some detail is not given about that vector. So Dot product of W and X is W.X = WT X
                     * Line in 2D,  plane in 3D and hyper plane in nD.
                     * Hyper plane in generalization of a concept of a line or plane in n-Dimension.
                     * Line 2D: y = mx + c, ax + by + c = 0, y = – c/b – ax/b
                     * Plane 3D: ax + by + cz + d = 0
                     * Hyper Plane n-D: W0 + W1X1 + W2X2 + W3X3 + ….. = 0
Summation formula:



Vector notation : 
                                          _   _
Wo + [Wo + W1 + W2 + …..+ Wn ] | X1 |    = 0        (do matrix multiplication)
                                         | X2 |
                                         | …. |
                                         | Xn |
                                         --   --


                     * So, if we have a vector Wnx1 and a vector Xnx1 (both column vector) so we write above formula like this:
Hyper plane (π):  WT X + W0= 0        (where T is transpose of a matrix)


                     * WT X + W0= 0 (W0 determines intercept of the hyperplane on y – axis)
                     * To pass through origin the intercept must be 0 which is :
( πn ) : WT X = 0  (W0 is zero)




                     * Let W and X are two vector then if equation is WT X = 0  (passing through origin):   
W.X = WT X = ||w|| ||x|| cos(θxy) ⇒ θ = 90 , because cos(90o) = 0 ⇒ W  X.


                        * Now if W is perpendicular then all the points in the x vector(which make a plane) are perpendicular to W.
                        * That means W Xi = 0        ( where Xi is all the points π(plane)  ).














Distance of a point from a plane/ Hyperplane, half-spaces
                           * Some source for this : lecture  blog
                           * ( πn ) : WT X = 0( Hyper plane passing through origin)  
                           * Angle between plane and W = 90o (W vector perpendicular to the plane π)
                           * Here θ < 90. So W.P is +ve.


                              * Now Distance(D) of a point P (p1, p2, …Pn) from a plane is:





⇒            (you can treat D as a component of P ⇒ Px= P cos)


⇒          


⇒           (abs because distance always positive)




                              * A hyper plane or a plane divides the whole space into two half spaces.
                              * For determining the point is in which half space of the plane: 
If W.P > 0 then point P lies in the direction of W(above the plane) and W.P < 0 then P lies opposite of W(below the plane. (Reasons because if P lies blow plane the the angle between W and P is  > 90 and cos(x)  where x> 90o is -ve).


Equation of a Circle (2-D), Sphere (3-D) and Hypersphere (n-D) :


                              * Circle: (x - a)2 + (y - b)2 = r2
                              * But , if centre is in origin then a and b become zero
x2 + y2 = c2
                              * Given a point p(x1,y1) : 
⇒ distance from the center: x12 + y12
So ,here is three cases:
                              * If distance from the center is less than radius then point lies inside a circle.
        x12 + y12 < radius.
                              * If distance from the center is more than radius then points lie outside the circle.
x12 + y12 > radius.
                              * If distance from the center is more than radius then points lie in the circumference of the circle.
x12 + y12 = radius.


Similarly, for sphere:
Sphere: x12 + x22 + x32 = r2         (where x1, x2, x3, are axis of a sphere)
Similarly, for Hyper sphere:
                              * Sphere: x12 + x22 + x32 +.....+ xn2 = r2         (where x1, x2, x3, …..xn are axis of a Hypersphere)


Summation formula: 

                              * If distance from the center is less than radius then point lies inside a hypersphere.
                              *  x12 + x22 + x32 +.....+ xn2 < r2.
                              * If distance from the center is more than radius then points lie outside the hypersphere.
                              *  x12 + x22 + x32 +.....+ xn2 > r2.
                              * If distance from the center is more than radius then points lie in the circumference of the hypersphere.
                              *  x12 + x22 + x32 +.....+ xn2 = r2.


  

(2D)Equation of an Ellipse, Ellipsoid and Hyper ellipsoid:


Ellipse: 


Three cases:


         (points lie inside ellipse)
         (points lie outside ellipse)
         (points coincide ellipse)


Ellipsoid (3D):



In nD Hyper Ellipsoid:







Square, rectangle:
  

                              * Here the rectangle is axis parallel  rectangle which is parallel to the axis.
                              * We can use simple if else condition to determine whether the points lie inside or outside of  the rectangle
                              * And the lines are called axis parallel lines.


Note::  Similarly above applied for cube, cuboid, hypercube, hyper cuboid.

Chapter 3: Introduction to Probability and Statistics
________________


Introduction:


                              * Datasets with well separable classes do not require ML methods to be applied, but classes that are well mixed cannot be separated easily.


                              * The data points that lie in the mixed region cannot be clearly stated to belong to a certain class. instead we can have probability values of the data point belonging to each class.
                              * Histograms, PDF, CDF, mean, variance, etc come under probability and statistics


                              * Random Variable: can take multiple values in a random manner
                              * Ex: Dice with 6 sides: Roll a fair dice: the 6 outcomes of the dice are equally likely r.v. X = {1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6}


                              * Discrete random variable: a value from a set of values
                              * Ex: Coin toss:. X = {H, T}


                              * Continuous random variable: a value from a range of values
                              * Ex: Height of a randomly picked student: Y = [120 to 190 cm]


Outlier: 
                              * Y: Height of students:
                              * Let: Y = {122.2, 146.4, 132.5, …, 12.26, 156.23, 92.6}
                              * Outliers: 92.6 and 12.26: may have occurred due to input error or data collection error or may be a genuine value but does not indicate general trend of the population.




Population and Sample:
                              * Population example: Set of heights(h) of all people in the world. 
                              * Task: estimate the average height of a human.
                              * Population Mean = (1/n) sum(h)
                              * Collecting all the population height data is not possible so, we will take a random sample (subset of the population) and do sample mean :
                              * Sample mean ((mean if often denoted by bar.)
                              * Let assume we take 1000 people randomly then:
                                 *  = mean of 1000 people randomly picked
                                    * As sample size increases our sample mean tends to become the Population mean.


Gaussian/Normal Distribution and its PDF (Probability Density Function):
                                    1. Why is probability zero for any given value of PDF
                                    2. Understand probability 
                                    3. PDF idea
                                    4. PMF, PDF and CDF in machine learning
                                    * Bell shaped curve: Gaussian distribution Probability density function curve
                                    * Distribution: it is a simple model of natural behaviour.
                                    * Real world variables mostly follow Gaussian distribution. ex:  Height, Weight, Length.
                                    * We learn distributions to understand the underlying statistics or trends of the population  
If X behaves as a Gaussian distribution: Given mean and standard variance. We can estimate the values of the population by plotting the PDF.
                                       * The red curve is standard normal distribution
                                       * Parameters of Gaussian distribution: Mean and variance.
                                       *  
                                          *  : follows
                                          * X: continuous random variable
                                          * N: normal or gaussian distribution
                                          * : mean of a distribution
                                          * : variance




  

                                             *  or P(X = x): PDF of normal(gaussian) distribution.
Observation:
                                                1. As x moves away from µ(mean) PDF reduces.
                                                2. The PDF of a Normal(gaussian) distribution is symmetric.
                                                3.                                                 * As variance increases pdf reduces both are inversely proportional.
                                                * In gaussian distribution the peak of the pdf is in the mean of the distribution.
                                                * The PDF of a Normal distribution is symmetric.
                                                * In a normal distribution, data around the mean occur more frequently.
                                                * The frequency of occurrence decreases the farther you are from the mean in a normal distribution.
  

CDF (Cumulative Distribution Function) of Gaussian/Normal distribution:
                                                * As variance is smaller the line is close(e.g: blue line)  to the mean and if variance is bigger the line is farther(e.g: orange line). 
                                                * Your mean lies on the 0.5 of y axis.
                                                * Let take x = 0.1 which corresponds to 0.7 in y axis it tells that:
                                                * 70% of your distribution is smaller than 0.1.
                                                * 30% of your distribution is bigger than 0.1.
                                                * As variance decreases the plot gets converted to a step function.




Standard deviation plot: 68 – 95 – 99.7 rule (article , article2 ):
                                                * Let assume our distribution which follows normal or gaussian distribution: 
                                                * N(µ,σ2) is X ~ N(0,4) 
                                                * So our variance(σ2) is 4 then our standard deviation(σ) is 2.
                                                *  Now if our standard deviation is 2 then between our first std deviation(σ) range we find 68% points.
                                                * Similarly in between 2σ 95% points and 3σ 99.7% points.
   


Symmetric distribution, Skewness and Kurtosis:
                                                * One of the applications of these stats is to understand the distribution.
                                                * Symmetric distribution:
                                                * a distribution which is similar in both sides e.g 50% of points is similar to other 50% of point e.g x2  graph.
                                                * If the PDF has a mirror image of the curve of the either sides of the mean, then the distribution is symmetric over mean; as in the above std dev plot.
                                                * F(x 0 – h) = F(x 0 + h): function symmetric over x 0
                                                * Skewness:
                                                *  tells about how far/ how dissimilar is a distribution from a symmetric distribution.
                                                * around the mean the distribution is not symmetric.one of the sides has a longer tail.
                                                * can be said to be a measure of asymmetry;
                                                * If the PDF has a mirror image of the curve of the either sides of the mean, then the
                                                * distribution is symmetric over mean; as in above std dev plot;
                                                * F(x 0 – h) = F(x 0 + h): function symmetric over x 0
                                                *   

                                                * Kurtosis( pronounce: ker-tau-sis):
                                                * Kurtosis is a measure of tailedness of the distribution.
                                                * Kurtosis of Gaussian random variable =3.
                                                * This will give us an idea of outliers in the distribution. Smaller the better.
                                                * Excess Kurtosis: Kurtosis – 3.
                                                * Through excess kurtosis we compare distributions with Gaussian random distribution.




  





Standard normal variate (z) and standardization:
                                                * If z is a standard normal variate then  z ~ N(0,1), where mean = 0 and variance = 1.
                                                * Let X ~ N(µ,σ2 ): this can be converted to Z(standard normal variate)  by:
                                                * Mean centering and scaling:  To help understand the disb.
                                                * So if we standardize the value then we know that 68% of our value lies between 1 and -1 from the 68 – 95 – 99.7 rule similarly 95% lies between -2 and 2.


Computing PDF from Histograms: using KDE:
                                                   * A Gaussian Kernel is plotted centered at each observation (of the histogram).
                                                   * Variance of this Kernel (bell curve) is called band width and bandwidth decreases the KDE become more rough and if increases the kde become more flat so we have to choose the bandwidth wishly.
                                                   * At every point in the range of the Gaussian Kernels we will add all PDF values to get a combined PDF.
                                                   * Bandwidth selection is done by experts.


 Comparison of the histogram (left) and kernel density estimate (right) constructed using the same data. The six individual kernels are the red dashed curves, the kernel density estimate the blue curves. The data points are the rug plot on the horizontal axis. 



Comparison of the histogram (left) and kernel density estimate (right) constructed using the same data. The six individual kernels are the red dashed curves, the kernel density estimates the blue curves. The data points are the rug plot on the horizontal axis.




Sampling distribution & Central Limit theorem(CLT):
Sampling distribution:
                                                   * Let X be a not necessarily Gaussian distribution of a population (say incomes)
                                                   * Pick a random Sample of independent size, let say n-size.
                                                   * Now you take m number of n-size sample:  S1, S2, S3, ..., Sm
                                                   * For all Samples compute their means, x1, x2, x3, ……xm.
                                                   * These sample means will have a distribution called Sampling distribution of sample-mean.
Central Limit Theorem(CTL):
        “Even if you are not normal...the average is normal” ~ from CTL ;-)


                                                   *  If X (original population) and X: finite mean and std. dev.
                                                   * Then Sampling distribution of sample means( x1,…xm.) will be a Gaussian distribution with:
                                                   * mean = population mean(µ)
                                                   * Std. dev. =  () as .
                                                      * It is generally observed or a thumb rule that if n  30, we can observe a Gaussian distribution.


Q-Q plot: 
How to test if a random variable is normally distributed or not?:
                                                         * Quantile – Quantile plot: Graphical way of determining similarity between two distributions.
                                                         * Y: a new distribution: Task: to understand whether Y is Gaussian or not:
                                                         * 1. Sort Y values and compute percentiles.
                                                         * 2. Take a standard gaussian distribution X ~ N(0,1): 
                                                         * take some k observations and sort + compute percentiles
                                                         * Std gaussian distribution is also called Theoretical quantiles.
                                                         * 3. Plot: Y percentile values on X axis and X percentile values on Y axis
                                                         * If the plot is approximately a straight line then the distributions are similar and distribution is gaussian or normal distribution.
#Q-Q plot
import numpy as np
import pylab
import scipy.stats as stats
​
# N(0,1) loc = mean, scale = standard deviation.
std_normal = np.random.normal(loc = 0, scale = 1, size=1000)
​
# 0 to 100th percentiles of std-normal
for i in range(0,101):
   print(i, np.percentile(std_normal,i))

.

How are distributions used?
                                                         * Probability is useful for data analysis.
                                                         * Example:
                                                         * Imagine your task is to order T-shirts for all employees at your company. Let sizes be S, M, L and XL. Say we have 100k employees who have different size requirements.
                                                         * Q) How many XL T-shirts should be ordered?
                                                         * Method 1:
                                                         * Collect data for all 100k employees which could be very time consuming.
                                                         * Method 2:
                                                         * Let us have a relationship between heights and T shirt size(Domain knowledge required)
                                                         * Collect heights from 500 random employees and Compute mean and std dev.
                                                         * From domain knowledge, let we found heights ~N(mean, variance)
                                                         * From this knowledge we can conclude many things.
                                                         * Q) Salaries: If salaries are Gaussian distributed, we can estimate how many employees make a salary > 100k $.
                                                         * So distributions give us a theoretical model of a random variable. This will help us understand the properties of the random variable.




Chebyshev’s (prono. : se-bi-sev ) inequality:
                                                         * If we don’t know the distribution and only know mean and standard deviation where mean is finite and std deviation is non-zero and finite then to find the percentage of values lie within a range :
                                                         * Example:
                                                         * Salaries of individuals (millions of values). distribution is unknown, but mean and std dev are known.
                                                         * Let assume mean and std dev are (40k, 10k) then:
                                                         * What percentage of individuals have a salary in the range of [20k , 60k] (2σ range).
                                                         * What percentage of individuals have a salary in the range of [10k , 70k] (3σ range).
                                                         * In probability theory, Chebyshev's inequality guarantees that, for a wide class of probability distributions, no more than a certain fraction of values can be more than a certain distance from the mean.
                                                         * That means that the probability of finding a point outside the range of X - kσ and X + kσ is less then .
 (where k is constant)


                                                            * For calculation video
                                                            * We can write above equation as :
 (where X is random variable)


                                                            * So from the above equation we can find that the probability of an individual salary in the range of [20k,60k] is greater than 75% .


Discrete and Continuous Uniform distributions:
                                                            * PDF for continuous and PMF for discrete random variables.
                                                            * Uniform distributions are probability distributions with equally likely outcomes.
                                                            * In a discrete uniform distribution, outcomes are discrete and have the same probability.
                                                            * In a continuous uniform distribution, outcomes are continuous and infinite.

Log-normal distribution:
In probability theory, a log-normal (or lognormal) distribution is a continuous probability distribution of a random variable whose logarithm is normally distributed or gaussian distribution. Thus, if the random variable X is log-normally distributed, then Y = ln(X) has a normal distribution.
                                                            * For finding if if X is log normally distributed or not:
                                                            * First we take Y = ln(X)
                                                            * And if Y is normally distributed then X is log normally distributed.




fig a (PDF), fig 2(Cumulative distribution function)

Application in human behaviour:
                                                            * The length of comments posted in Internet discussion forums follows a log-normal distribution.
                                                            * Users dwell time on online articles (jokes, news etc.) follows a log-normal distribution.
                                                            * The length of chess games tends to follow a log-normal distribution








power law:  
In statistics, a power law is a functional relationship between two quantities, where a relative change in one quantity results in a proportional relative change in the other quantity, independent of the initial size of those quantities: one quantity varies as a power of another.


When a distribution follows a power law then the distribution is called Pareto distribution.


Parameters: scale and shape




Pareto distribution:
The Pareto distribution, named after the Italian civil engineer, economist, and sociologist Vilfredo Pareto is a power-law probability distribution that is used in description of social, quality control, scientific, geophysical, actuarial, and many other types of observable phenomena. The Pareto principle or "80-20 rule" stating that 80% of outcomes are due to 20% of causes. Empirical observation has shown that this 80-20 distribution fits a wide range of cases, including natural phenomena and human activities.


fig a (PDF), fig 2(CDF)
  
  

Search its perimeter and mean etc. in wikipedia.


  

                                                            * If the log-log plot shows a straight line then it is a power law plot. Log log plot helps us to determine if the plot is a power law plot or not.
                                                            * We can also use a QQ plot against Pareto plot.


Box cox transform:
                                                            * In Machine Learning we assume generally that a feature follows a Gaussian distribution can be easily analyzed because gaussian distribution has many research.


                                                            * We can convert Pareto into Gaussian by using Power transform :
                                                            * In statistics, a power transform is a family of functions applied to create a monotonic transformation of data using power functions.
                                                            * Pareto: Xi:  x1, x2, …., xn .
                                                            * We can use box-cox which gives lambda for detail search in wikipedia:
                                                            * boxcox(Xi) = λ
                                                            * It gives a lambda value and we can use this to convert perito distribution to normal distribution by using power transformation:


        
                                                            * If λ = 0, then the power law distribution is actually a log normal.
                                                            * Yi = scipy.stats.boxcox(xi)
                                                            * Return a dataset transformed by a Box-Cox power transformation.
                                                            * [source]


                                                            * Box-cox does not always work. Use QQ-plot to check its results.


Applications of non-Gaussian distributions:
                                                            * Suppose if you have a 200 sample data of rainfalls in a particular area and if you don’t have 20cm data in it because you can’t have enough data then you estimate that there is 0 probability of raining more than 20cm This is wrong so we find well studied distribution to fit in that 200 value so we can get real information from that.
                                                            * It is more trustworthy to use a well studied distribution rather than a direct method like percentile, mean, mode , std etc.
                                                            * We use Uniform distribution for generating random numbers.
                                                            * A well studied distribution gives a theoretical model for how the random variable or data is distributed.


                                                            * Weibull distributions source:
                                                            * The upstream rainfall determines the height of a dam which stands for 100 years without repairs.
                                                            * This distribution is applied to extreme events such as annual maximum one day rainfalls and river discharges to find how high a dam should be.


Steps to find which distribution fit in a random variable Xi
                                                            1. First find which distribution fits by the help of QQ plot, K-S test , etc.
                                                            2. Obtain parameters and convert random variables to distribution.
                                                            3. Now you have a Yi which is a  well studied distribution.
                                                            4. Now you can find its pdf,cdf and mean etc.


Co-variance:
                                                            * Task: To determine relationships between two random variables.
                                                            * Cov(x, y) = (1 / n) * sum((xi - µx ) (yi - µy ))
                                                            * Cov(x, x) = variance of x.
                                                            * Cov(x, y) will be positive if as x increases y increases
                                                            * Cov(x, y) will be negative if as x increases y decreases
                                                            * Sign of covariance is indicative of relationship.
                                                            * The value of covariance does not indicate strength of relationship and if we change meter to km of the same data set then our co-variance will change. This is the major drawback of covariance.


Pearson Correlation Coefficient (PCC):
To measure relationship between two random variables:
   (cov is covariance and σx , σy is std deviations of x and y).


ρ = 0  no relation between the two random variables.






  

                                                            * From above you can deduce that the relation in x and y leads to different values of ρ.
                                                            * ρ = 0  no relation between the two random variables.
                                                            * Pearson correlation coefficient assumes that the relationship is linear.


Draw backs:


  





                                                            * PCC Does not perform well with non-linear relationships.
                                                            * This plot has a monotonically non decreasing curve.














Spearman Rank Correlation Coefficient (source):
  

                                                            * Pearson corr coeff: works for linear relationships. But Spearman rank corr coeff work for monotonic relationships (whether linear or not).
                                                            * Spearman correlation coefficient is much more robust to outliers than Pearson Corr coef.
                                                            * This allows us to understand whether two random variables increase at the same time.














  
  

This is Pearson correlation coefficient of ranks of the random variables.


  







Correlation vs Causation:
Correlation does not imply causation.
                                                            * Example: 
                                                            * Number of Nobel Laureates vs Chocolate consumption per capita.
                                                            * The rank correlation coefficient ~ 0.8
                                                            * As x increases y increases.
                                                            * But we know that chocolate consumption and Number of Nobel Laureates do not relate.
                                                            * Causations are studied with causal models which is a separate field of study.




How to use correlations?
                                                            * Q. Is salary correlated with sq footage of home?
                                                            * This data will be useful for real estate agents;
                                                            * Q. Is the number of years of education correlated with income?
                                                            * Useful for education ministry to encourage people to get more years of study;
                                                            * Q. Is time spent on a web page in the last 24 hours correlated with money spent in the next 24 hours?
                                                            * Useful for ecommerce to encourage people to spend more time on the website.
                                                            * Q. Is the number of unique visitors to the website correlated with the $ sales in a day?
                                                            * The company will then take measures to increase the number of unique users in a day.
                                                            * Q. Dosage correlation with reduction in blood sugar?
                                                            * Correlations can help us answer these questions.




Confidence interval (C.I) Introduction:
                                                            * Let X be a random variable of height values.
                                                            * Distribution of X is unknown.
                                                            * Task: Estimate the population mean of the distribution X:
                                                            * As the number of sample mean sizes increases, sample mean reaches population mean.
                                                            * With x_bar we have a point estimate and x_bar is a sample mean that is calculated over a randomly selected sample of the population.
                                                            * Instead we can also express mean in terms of confidence interval
                                                            * Let x_bar represent the sample mean and μ represent the population mean. Now, if we repeat the sampling multiple times, each time, we get a different value of sample mean, x_bar. 
                                                            * In 95% of the sampling experiments, μ will be between the endpoints of the C.I calculated using x_bar, but in 5% of the cases, it will not.
                                                            * 95% C.I does NOT mean that μ lies in the interval with a probability of 95%.        
                                                            * Confidence Interval: An N% confidence interval for parameter p is an interval that includes p with probability N%; [Source: Tom Mitchell book]






Computing confidence interval given the underlying distribution:
                                                            * Imagine we have a random variable of heights of population.
                                                            * Let this random variable follow a Gaussian distribution;
                                                            * Let mean = 168 cm and standard deviation = 5
                                                            * We can get 95% CI from Gaussian distribution characteristics which range from   
[ μ - 2σ, μ + 2σ ]  which is 158 to 178 cm.




















C.I for population mean of a random variable:
                                                               * Let X be some random variable belong to any distribution X ~ F(μ, σ2)
                                                               * Let sample size(n) be 10.
                                                               * What is the 95% CI for population mean, the distribution can be anything:
                                                               * Case 1: if we know the std dev Let σ = 5cm
                                                               * From CTL : 
                                                               * sample mean (  )  = population mean(μ)
                                                               * Sample standard deviation = 
                                                               * We can say that μ  [ - 2   + 2] with 95% confidence.
                                                               * So if the sample is {180, 162, 158, 172, 168, 150, 171, 183, 165, 176}
                                                               * Population Mean lies between 165.34 and 171.66 with 95% confidence.  


                                                                  * Case 2: if we do not know the std dev
                                                                  * In this case we use student t-distribution:
                                                                  * Biggest application of student t-distribution is to calculate the confidence interval when σ is not given.
                                                                  * v is a parameter of t-distribution called degree of freedom as p increases peakedness increases.
                                                                  * ν = sample size – 1
                                                                  * Now we can apply the property of t-distribution and find 95% of confidence interval.








                                                                  * We have CI for mean, what if we like to have CI for standard deviation, median, 90th percentile and other statistics, we can use bootstrap C.I with the help of computer computation power.


Confidence interval using bootstrapping:
                                                                  * Let X be a random variable with unknown distribution F
                                                                  * Let S be a sample from distribution X
                                                                  * sample size n {x1, x2, …, xn}
                                                                  * From sample S we generate k number of new samples(s1, s2 ....,sk ) with size m:
                                                                  * s1 : x1(1), x2(1), …., xm(1) 
.
.
sk : x1(k), x2(k), …., xm(k)  where, m <= n
We take m * k grid


                                                                  * We can generate k samples (s1, s2 ....,sk) from sample S with the help of a discrete uniform random variable generator.
                                                                  * Taking samples from the Sample S some value will repeat called sample with replacement.
                                                                  * And the samples s1, s2 ....,sk  called  bootstrap-samples.
                                                                  * Considering median: 
                                                                  *  Let k be 1000
                                                                  * we can have k medians from k samples
                                                                  * Sort these k medians in increasing order
                                                                  * 95% CI for population median is  from [median 25 , median 975 ]
                                                                  * For the same above process we can generate any statistic desired e.g median, std dev, percentile etc.
                                                                  * This is a non parametric technique(doesn't make any assumptions about the distribution) to estimate CI for a statistic and this process is called a bootstrap sampling technique.


Sample code:

Empirical bootstrap based Confidence Interval


import numpy
from matplotlib import pyplot
from pandas import read_csv
from sklearn.utils import resample
from sklearn.metrics import accuracy_score


# load data set x is sample distribution
x = numpy.array([180,162,158,172,168,150,171,183,165,176])


# configure bootstrap
# size of bootstrap sample k
k = 1000
# size of samples is m which is equal size of x because m <= n
m = int(len(x))


# creating 1000 bootstrap samples and computing median
median = list()
for i in range(k):
#     resample(sample_of_distribution , resampling_size)
   s = resample(x, n_samples = m)
   median_of_s = numpy.median(s)
   median.append(median_of_s)


pyplot.hist(median)
pyplot.show()
​
# we want 95% C.I so, alpha = 0.95
alpha = 0.95
​
# lower and upper  percentage
lower_percentage = ((1.0-alpha)/2.0) * 100
upper_percentage = (alpha + (1.0 - alpha)/2.0) * 100
print(lower_percentage)
print(upper_percentage)


# upper and lower limit of distribution
lower = numpy.percentile(median, lower_percentage)
upper = numpy.percentile(median, upper_percentage)
​
print('%.1f confidence interval %.1f and %.1f' % (alpha * 100, lower, upper))

95.0 confidence interval 161.5 and 176.0




Hypothesis testing methodology, Null-hypothesis, p-value:
                                                                  * Let us have 2 classes, and sample heights of students in both classes is size 50 each. I.e c1 has 50 samples and c2 has 50 samples.
                                                                  * Q. Is there a difference in heights of students in c1 and c2?
                                                                  * Say we plot histograms and see that the mean of c2 is greater than the mean of c1.
                                                                  * Let us observe that μ2 – μ1 >= 10cm. (THIS OBSERVATION IS TRUE )
                                                                  * Hypothesis testing ( explanation video statquest video):
                                                                  * 1. Choosing a test statistic: (μ2 – μ1) (difference in height)
                                                                  * Let X be our test statistic.
                                                                  * 2. Define a Null hypothesis (H0 ): 
                                                                  * there is no difference in class of heights
                                                                  * μ2 – μ1 = 0.
                                                                  * Define a Alternative hypothesis (H1 ):
                                                                  * there exists a difference in class of heights
                                                                  * 3. Compute p-value for above hypothesis:
                                                                  * P value:  probability of observing a value of X>= 10cm if there was no difference in the class height (null hypothesis).
                                                                  * Say we have p_value = 0.9 of observing a mean difference(μ2 – μ1) of 10cm.
                                                                  * Then the probability of finding the difference 10cm is 90% if H0 is true.
                                                                  * So we reject the Alternative hypothesis (H1 ) and accept H0.
                                                                  * Say we have p_value = 0.02 of observing a mean difference(μ2 – μ1) of 10cm.
                                                                  * Then the probability of finding the difference 10cm is 2% if H0 is true.
                                                                  * So we reject the H0 and accept the Alternative hypothesis (H1 ). 


“When H0 is true,As p value is higher than a threshold level (generally 5%) then we accept the null hypothesis else we reject the null hypothesis.
Also this p value does not tell about the acceptance of alternate hypotheses or the probability of acceptance of null hypothesis.
Take an observation of μ2 – μ1 from the classes which is equal to 10, that is μ2 – μ1 = 10 the ground truth statistic that has already occurred. and computing the p
value as the conditional probability of finding μ2 – μ1 = 10 given H0 (which says
that there is no difference in means), if the p value is less than threshold that is
5% we reject null hypothesis saying that we cannot accept that there is no
difference in means and we reject null hypothesis in favour of the alternate
hypothesis; if the p value is > 5% we say that we don’t have sufficient the null
hypothesis saying that there is sufficient evidence that the observed value occurs
under the null hypothesis with satisfactory probability.”


Note: P value says that what is the probability of an observation statistic occurring under null hypothesis.


Hypothesis testing intuition with coin toss example:
                                                                  * Task:         Given a coin determines if the coin is biased towards heads or not.
                                                                  * Biased towards head: P(H )> 0.5
                                                                  * Not biased: P(H) = 0.5.
                                                                  * Design an experiment: flip a coin 5 times and count the number of heads = X   ,where X is a random variable which is equal to the number of heads.
                                                                  * This X is the test statistic (the number of heads)
                                                                  * Perform experiment: Let that we got 5 heads
                                                                  * test statistic X = 5 the observed value.
                                                                  * Let the null hypothesis be “coin is not biased towards head”
                                                                  * P(X=5 | coin is not biased towards head) = ?
                                                                  * Probability of observed value given null hypothesis:
                                                                  * P(X=5|H0 ) = (½) 5 ~ 0.03
                                                                  * P(X=5|H0 ) = 0.03 < 0.05
                                                                  * Recap:
                                                                  * p –value = Probability of observing an observed value given a null hypothesisIf.
                                                                  * p-value < 0.05 then null hypothesis is incorrect .
                                                                  * observation is ground truth cannot be incorrect, we then reject null hypothesis in favor of alternative hypothesis.
                                                                  * We reject that the coin is unbiased and accept that the coin is biased.
                                                                  * We have sample size as a choice. We can have 5 coin flips, we can have 3 flips, 10 flips or 100 flips and can perform the experiment.
                                                                  * Let we flips five times coin and observe 5 Heads {H, H, H, H, H}
                                                                  * Test statistic(X) : number of heads.
                                                                  * P-value: probability( X = 5 | h0 is true )
                                                                  * p-value = ½ * ½ * ½ * ½ * ½ =  = 0.03125 ~ 3% and it is less than 5%.
                                                                  * Thus we reject the null hypothesis and accept alternative hypotheses.
                                                                  * There is a 3% chance of getting 5 heads in five flips if the coin is not biased towards heads.
                                                                     * Important criterion for hypothesis testing:
                                                                     * 1. Design of the experiment : sample size, experiment ...etc.
                                                                     * 2. Defining Null Hypothesis:make null hypothesis easy.
                                                                     * 3. Design Test statistic : e.g: (μ2 – μ1)
                                                                     * Error people make:
                                                                     * P-value is the probability of observation given H0, we cannot say anything about the probability of H0 being true.


                                                                     * Note that failure to reject H0 does not mean the null hypothesis is true. There is no formal outcome that says “accept H0 .” It only means that we do not have sufficient evidence to support H1.
                                                                     * Example:
                                                                     * In a jury trial the hypotheses are:
                                                                     * • H0 : defendant is innocent
                                                                     * • H1 : defendant is guilty. 
                                                                     * H0 (innocent) is rejected if H1 (guilty) is supported by evidence beyond “reasonable doubt.” Failure to reject H0 (prove guilty) does not imply innocence, only that the evidence is insufficient to reject it.
                                                                     * 



Re-sampling and permutation test:
                                                                     * We have the same student heights.Take means and compute the difference between the means, let this difference be Δ.
                                                                     * Test statistic (X): difference of mean (μ2 – μ1)
                                                                     * Then p-value is : probability  (X > Δ | if h0 is true).
                                                                     * We then mix all height values into a 100 value vector and randomly sample 50 points from the 100 points to form the X vector and rest 50 into the Y vector.
                                                                     * Now We have a new means of X and Y.
                                                                     * The difference of these means is the mean difference of Sample_1 say δ1.
                                                                     * Similarly we will generate n samples and compute mean differences, we will have n mean differences.
                                                                     * Note: use random sampling for picking 50 points in X and Y class.
                                                                     * Let n = 10000
                                                                     * The mean differences are sorted. 
                                                                     * δ1, δ2, …., δ10000 in sorted order.
                                                                     * Now place the observed mean( Δ ) difference before sampling in the sorted means list.
                                                                     * I.e:  δ1, δ2, …,δ9500,  Δ,  δ9501 ,..., δ10000 
                                                                     * Computing the percentage of δi values that are greater Δ  will generate a p-value. Say Δ  ~ δ9500, then we have 500 δi  values (from δ9501 to δ10000 ) that are greater than the observed mean( Δ ) .
                                                                     * Hence we have 5% of values.
                                                                     * Hence p-value = 5%.
                                                                     * We can compute p-value with this process.


                                                                     * Why is this percentage considered as p-value:
                                                                     * Initially while jumbling we assumed that there is no difference in the means of the 2 classes or 2 height lists, this is the assumption of null hypothesis. Then we have experimented with sampling for 10000 iterations.If the percentage of the sorted sample differences that are greater than original mean difference D is less than threshold value 5% then the sampling is not random this implies that null hypothesis should be rejected. p-value thresholds depend on the problem. For medical purposes p-value of 1% is reasonable, generally p-value threshold is kept at 5%.


K-S( Kolmogorov-Smirnov ) Test for similarity of two distributions:
                                                                     * Docs for ks test
                                                                     * It is the application of hypothesis testing. it uses the idea of hypothesis testing like null hypothesis, etc. to determine if two random variables are from the same distribution or not.
                                                                     * Do two random variables X1 and X2 follow the same distribution?
                                                                     * We plot CDF for both random variables.
  

                                                                     * We will be using hypothesis testing:
                                                                     * Null hypothesis(H0: the two random variables come from the same distribution.
                                                                     * Alternative hypothesis: both don’t come from the same distribution.
                                                                     * Test statistic (Dn,m) : difference between cdf of two random variable at same value of x.
                                                                     *    
                                                                     * n and m are the size of X1 and X2.
                                                                     * sup is supremum : maximum difference, black arrow in above image.
                                                                     * Dn,m is a test statistic.
                                                                        * Dn,m: max of ( CDF(X1 distribution) – CDF(X2 distribution) ) (at same value on x axis)
                                                                        * Now, let our p-value is 0.05, also called the significance level of alpha α = 0.05.
                                                                        * If  then we reject H0 at a 5% or 0.05 significance level.
                                                                        *                                                                           * E.g:
                                                                           * n = 1000, m = 5000
                                                                           * P-value of significance of alpha (α) is : 0.5
                                                                           * we get c(α) = 1.358
                                                                           * Now computing from the above formula we get 0.0470.
                                                                           * If Dn,m > 0.0470:
        reject H0 at 0.05 significance level.
NOTE: 
                                                                           1. In general hypothesis testing H0 is rejected @ a significance level of alpha (α) if:
p-value  <  α


                                                                           2. In KS test H0 is rejected if:
                                                                           * 

Ks test code:
